Common applications include molecular encapsulation, selective ion or molecule transport, catalysis (as nanoreactors), gas storage (e.g., clathrate hydrates), and drug delivery systems. Their rigid, enclosed cavities make them especially suited for applications requiring selective binding and protection of guest molecules.\n\nMacrocycles, on the other hand, are large ring-shaped molecules (such as crown ethers, cyclodextrins, and porphyrins) that often feature a central cavity or binding site. They are widely used in ion recognition and transport (e.g., selective binding of metal cations), as molecular sensors, in catalysis, and as building blocks for larger supramolecular assemblies. Macrocycles are also important in medicinal chemistry (e.g., as antibiotics like vancomycin) and materials science.\n\nThere is overlap in their uses, particularly in host-guest chemistry, molecular recognition, and catalysis. Both can act as hosts for ions or small molecules, and both are used in the design of molecular machines and sensors. However, cage molecules are generally preferred when a fully enclosed environment is needed for the guest, while macrocycles are favored for applications requiring easier guest exchange or planar binding sites.\n\nIn summary, while both cage molecules and macrocycles are central to supramolecular chemistry and share some applications, their structural differences lead to distinct and sometimes Common applications include molecular encapsulation, selective ion or molecule transport, catalysis (as nanoreactors), gas storage (e.g., clathrate hydrates), and drug delivery systems. Their rigid, enclosed cavities make them especially suited for applications requiring selective binding and protection of guest molecules.\n\nMacrocycles, on the other hand, are large ring-shaped molecules (such as crown ethers, cyclodextrins, and porphyrins) that often feature a central cavity or binding site. They are widely used in ion recognition and transport (e.g., selective binding of metal cations), as molecular sensors, in catalysis, and as building blocks for larger supramolecular assemblies. Macrocycles are also important in medicinal chemistry (e.g., as antibiotics like vancomycin) and materials science.\n\nThere is overlap in their uses, particularly in host-guest chemistry, molecular recognition, and catalysis. Both can act as hosts for ions or small molecules, and both are used in the design of molecular machines and sensors. However, cage molecules are generally preferred when a fully enclosed environment is needed for the guest, while macrocycles are favored for applications requiring easier guest exchange or planar binding sites.\n\nIn summary, while both cage molecules and macrocycles are central to supramolecular chemistry and share some applications, their structural differences lead to distinct and sometimes Common applications include molecular encapsulation, selective ion or molecule transport, catalysis (as nanoreactors), gas storage (e.g., clathrate hydrates), and drug delivery systems. Their rigid, enclosed cavities make them especially suited for applications requiring selective binding and protection of guest molecules.\n\nMacrocycles, on the other hand, are large ring-shaped molecules (such as crown ethers, cyclodextrins, and porphyrins) that often feature a central cavity or binding site. They are widely used in ion recognition and transport (e.g., selective binding of metal cations), as molecular sensors, in catalysis, and as building blocks for larger supramolecular assemblies. Macrocycles are also important in medicinal chemistry (e.g., as antibiotics like vancomycin) and materials science.\n\nThere is overlap in their uses, particularly in host-guest chemistry, molecular recognition, and catalysis. binding sites.\n\nIn summary, while both cage molecules and macrocycles are central to supramolecular chemistry and share some applications, their structural differences lead to distinct and sometimes
